<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Argon</h1>			<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
				<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>				<div id="contentSub"></div>
				<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>				<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
				<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
				<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">This article is about the chemical element. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Argon_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Argon (disambiguation)">Argon (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">

</p>
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">chemical element with atomic number 18</div><table class="infobox" style="width:22em"><caption><span style="white-space:nowrap">Argon,&#160;&#160;<sub><span style="font-size:smaller;">18</span></sub>Ar</span></caption><tbody><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">
<a href="/wiki/File:Argon_discharge_tube.jpg" class="image" title="Vial containing a violet glowing gas"><img alt="Vial containing a violet glowing gas" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Argon_discharge_tube.jpg/220px-Argon_discharge_tube.jpg" width="220" height="147" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Argon_discharge_tube.jpg/330px-Argon_discharge_tube.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Argon_discharge_tube.jpg/440px-Argon_discharge_tube.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3008" data-file-height="2008" /></a></td></tr><tr><th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;background:#c0ffff">General properties</th></tr><tr><th scope="row">Pronunciation</th><td>
<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="/ɑːr/: &#39;ar&#39; in &#39;far&#39;">ɑːr</span><span title="/ɡ/: &#39;g&#39; in &#39;guy&#39;">ɡ</span><span title="/ɒ/: &#39;o&#39; in &#39;body&#39;">ɒ</span><span title="&#39;n&#39; in &#39;nigh&#39;">n</span></span>/</a></span></span>&#x20;<wbr />&#8203;<span class="nowrap">(<a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i title="English pronunciation respelling"><span style="font-size:90%">AR</span>-gon</i></a>)</span></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Appearance</th><td>
colorless gas exhibiting a lilac/violet glow when placed in an electric field</td></tr><tr><th scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Standard_atomic_weight" title="Standard atomic weight">Standard atomic weight</a><span style="font-weight:normal;">&#x20;(<i>A</i><sub>r,&#160;standard</sub>)</span></th><td>

<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Infobox_argon" title="Template:Infobox argon"><span title="View this template">view</span></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Infobox_argon" title="Template talk:Infobox argon"><span title="Discuss this template">talk</span></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Infobox_argon&amp;action=edit"><span title="Edit this template">edit</span></a></li></ul></div>&#x20;|&#x20;<a href="/wiki/List_of_data_references_for_chemical_elements" title="List of data references for chemical elements">references</a></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Argon</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">chemical element</a> with symbol&#160;<b>Ar</b> and <a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">atomic number</a>&#160;18. It is in group 18 of the <a href="/wiki/Periodic_table" title="Periodic table">periodic table</a> and is a <a href="/wiki/Noble_gas" title="Noble gas">noble gas</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Argon is the third-most abundant gas in the <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_atmosphere" class="mw-redirect" title="Earth&#39;s atmosphere">Earth's atmosphere</a>, at 0.934% (9340 <a href="/wiki/Parts-per_notation" title="Parts-per notation">ppmv</a>). It is more than twice as abundant as <a href="/wiki/Water_vapor" title="Water vapor">water vapor</a> (which averages about 4000 ppmv, but varies greatly), 23 times as abundant as <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> (400 ppmv), and more than 500 times as abundant as <a href="/wiki/Neon" title="Neon">neon</a> (18 ppmv). Argon is the most abundant noble gas in Earth's crust, comprising 0.00015% of the crust.
</p><p>Nearly all of the argon in the Earth's atmosphere is <a href="/wiki/Radiogenic" class="mw-redirect" title="Radiogenic">radiogenic</a> <a href="/wiki/Argon-40" class="mw-redirect" title="Argon-40">argon-40</a>, derived from the <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_decay" title="Radioactive decay">decay</a> of <a href="/wiki/Potassium-40" title="Potassium-40">potassium-40</a> in the Earth's crust. In the universe, <a href="/wiki/Argon-36" class="mw-redirect" title="Argon-36">argon-36</a> is by far the most common argon <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotope</a>, as it is the most easily produced by stellar <a href="/wiki/Nucleosynthesis" title="Nucleosynthesis">nucleosynthesis</a> in <a href="/wiki/Supernova" title="Supernova">supernovas</a>.
</p><p>The name "argon" is derived from the <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> word <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">ἀργόν</span>, neuter singular form of <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">ἀργός</span> meaning "lazy" or "inactive", as a reference to the fact that the element undergoes almost no chemical reactions. The complete <a href="/wiki/Octet_rule" title="Octet rule">octet</a> (eight electrons) in the outer atomic shell makes argon stable and resistant to bonding with other elements. Its <a href="/wiki/Triple_point" title="Triple point">triple point</a> temperature of 83.8058&#160;<a href="/wiki/Kelvin" title="Kelvin">K</a> is a defining fixed point in the <a href="/wiki/International_Temperature_Scale_of_1990" title="International Temperature Scale of 1990">International Temperature Scale of 1990</a>.
</p><p>Argon is produced industrially by the <a href="/wiki/Fractional_distillation" title="Fractional distillation">fractional distillation</a> of <a href="/wiki/Liquid_air" title="Liquid air">liquid air</a>. Argon is mostly used as an <a href="/wiki/Inert_gas" title="Inert gas">inert</a> <a href="/wiki/Shielding_gas" title="Shielding gas">shielding gas</a> in welding and other high-temperature industrial processes where ordinarily unreactive substances become reactive; for example, an argon atmosphere is used in <a href="/wiki/Graphite" title="Graphite">graphite</a> electric furnaces to prevent the graphite from burning. Argon is also used in <a href="/wiki/Incandescent_light_bulb" title="Incandescent light bulb">incandescent</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fluorescent_lighting" class="mw-redirect" title="Fluorescent lighting">fluorescent lighting</a>, and other gas-discharge tubes. Argon makes a distinctive <a href="/wiki/Ion_laser#Argon_laser" title="Ion laser">blue-green gas laser</a>. Argon is also used in fluorescent glow starters.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Characteristics">Characteristics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Argon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Characteristics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Argon_ice_1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Argon_ice_1.jpg/170px-Argon_ice_1.jpg" width="170" height="152" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Argon_ice_1.jpg/255px-Argon_ice_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Argon_ice_1.jpg/340px-Argon_ice_1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1829" data-file-height="1632" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Argon_ice_1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A small piece of rapidly melting solid argon</div></div></div>
<p>Argon has approximately the same <a href="/wiki/Solubility" title="Solubility">solubility</a> in water as oxygen and is 2.5 times more soluble in water than <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a>. Argon is colorless, odorless, nonflammable and nontoxic as a solid, liquid or gas.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Argon is chemically <a href="/wiki/Inert_gas" title="Inert gas">inert</a> under most conditions and forms no confirmed stable compounds at room temperature.
</p><p>Although argon is a <a href="/wiki/Noble_gas" title="Noble gas">noble gas</a>, it can form some compounds under various extreme conditions. <a href="/wiki/Argon_fluorohydride" title="Argon fluorohydride">Argon fluorohydride</a> (HArF), a compound of argon with <a href="/wiki/Fluorine" title="Fluorine">fluorine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> that is stable below 17&#160;K (−256.1&#160;°C; −429.1&#160;°F), has been demonstrated.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-sciencenews-harf_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sciencenews-harf-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>  Although the neutral ground-state chemical compounds of argon are presently limited to HArF, argon can form <a href="/wiki/Clathrates" class="mw-redirect" title="Clathrates">clathrates</a> with <a href="/wiki/Water_(molecule)" class="mw-redirect" title="Water (molecule)">water</a> when atoms of argon are trapped in a lattice of water molecules.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ions" class="mw-redirect" title="Ions">Ions</a>, such as <span class="chemf nowrap">ArH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span>, and <a href="/wiki/Exciplex" class="mw-redirect" title="Exciplex">excited-state complexes</a>, such as ArF, have been demonstrated. Theoretical calculation predicts several more argon compounds that should be stable<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> but have not yet been synthesized.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Argon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Isolation_of_Argon.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Isolation_of_Argon.png/170px-Isolation_of_Argon.png" width="170" height="383" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Isolation_of_Argon.png/255px-Isolation_of_Argon.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Isolation_of_Argon.png/340px-Isolation_of_Argon.png 2x" data-file-width="357" data-file-height="804" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Isolation_of_Argon.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/John_William_Strutt,_3rd_Baron_Rayleigh" title="John William Strutt, 3rd Baron Rayleigh">Lord Rayleigh</a>'s method for the isolation of argon, based on an experiment of <a href="/wiki/Henry_Cavendish" title="Henry Cavendish">Henry Cavendish</a>'s. The gases are contained in a test-tube (A) standing over a large quantity of weak <a href="/wiki/Alkali" title="Alkali">alkali</a> (B), and the current is conveyed in wires insulated by U-shaped glass tubes (CC) passing through the liquid and round the mouth of the test-tube. The inner platinum ends (DD) of the wire receive a current from a battery of five <a href="/wiki/Grove_cell" title="Grove cell">Grove cells</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Ruhmkorff_coil" class="mw-redirect" title="Ruhmkorff coil">Ruhmkorff coil</a> of medium size.</div></div></div>
<p><i>Argon</i> (<a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">ἀργόν</span>, neuter singular form of <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">ἀργός</span> meaning "lazy" or "inactive"), is named in reference to its chemical inactivity. This chemical property of this first <a href="/wiki/Noble_gas" title="Noble gas">noble gas</a> to be discovered impressed the namers.<sup id="cite_ref-lazyone1_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lazyone1-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-lazyone2_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lazyone2-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> An unreactive gas was suspected to be a component of air by <a href="/wiki/Henry_Cavendish" title="Henry Cavendish">Henry Cavendish</a> in 1785. Argon was first isolated from air in 1894 by <a href="/wiki/John_William_Strutt,_3rd_Baron_Rayleigh" title="John William Strutt, 3rd Baron Rayleigh">Lord Rayleigh</a> and Sir <a href="/wiki/William_Ramsay" title="William Ramsay">William Ramsay</a> at <a href="/wiki/University_College_London" title="University College London">University College London</a> by removing <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a>, water, and <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a> from a sample of clean air.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-lazyone3_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lazyone3-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> They had determined that nitrogen produced from chemical compounds was 0.5% lighter than nitrogen from the atmosphere. The difference was slight, but it was important enough to attract their attention for many months. They concluded that there was another gas in the air mixed in with the nitrogen.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> Argon was also encountered in 1882 through independent research of H. F. Newall and W. N. Hartley.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Each observed new lines in the <a href="/wiki/Emission_spectrum" title="Emission spectrum">emission spectrum</a> of air that did not match known elements.
</p><p>Until 1957, the symbol for argon was "A", but now is "Ar".<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Occurrence">Occurrence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Argon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Occurrence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Argon constitutes 0.934% by volume and 1.288% by mass of the <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_atmosphere" class="mw-redirect" title="Earth&#39;s atmosphere">Earth's atmosphere</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> and air is the primary industrial source of purified argon products. Argon is isolated from air by fractionation, most commonly by <a href="/wiki/Cryogenics" title="Cryogenics">cryogenic</a> <a href="/wiki/Fractional_distillation" title="Fractional distillation">fractional distillation</a>, a process that also produces purified <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neon" title="Neon">neon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Krypton" title="Krypton">krypton</a> and <a href="/wiki/Xenon" title="Xenon">xenon</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> The Earth's crust and seawater contain 1.2 ppm and 0.45 ppm of argon, respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-emsley_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-emsley-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Isotopes">Isotopes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Argon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Isotopes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_argon" title="Isotopes of argon">Isotopes of argon</a></div>
<p>The main <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotopes</a> of argon found on Earth are <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">40</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Ar</span> (99.6%), <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">36</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Ar</span> (0.34%), and <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">38</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Ar</span> (0.06%). Naturally occurring <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">40</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span><a href="/wiki/Potassium" title="Potassium">K</a></span>, with a <a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">half-life</a> of 1.25<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup><span style="white-space:nowrap">9</span></sup> years, decays to stable <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">40</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Ar</span> (11.2%) by <a href="/wiki/Electron_capture" title="Electron capture">electron capture</a> or <a href="/wiki/Positron_emission" title="Positron emission">positron emission</a>, and also to stable <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">40</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Ca</span> (88.8%) by <a href="/wiki/Beta_decay" title="Beta decay">beta decay</a>. These properties and ratios are used to determine the age of <a href="/wiki/Rock_(geology)" title="Rock (geology)">rocks</a> by <a href="/wiki/K%E2%80%93Ar_dating" title="K–Ar dating">K–Ar dating</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-emsley_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-emsley-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-iso_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iso-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the Earth's atmosphere, <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">39</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Ar</span> is made by <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_ray" title="Cosmic ray">cosmic ray</a> activity, primarily by neutron capture of <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">40</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Ar</span> followed by two-neutron emission. In the subsurface environment, it is also produced through <a href="/wiki/Neutron_capture" title="Neutron capture">neutron capture</a> by <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">39</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>K</span>, followed by proton emission. <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">37</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Ar</span> is created from the <a href="/wiki/Neutron_capture" title="Neutron capture">neutron capture</a> by <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">40</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Ca</span> followed by an <a href="/wiki/Alpha_particle" title="Alpha particle">alpha particle</a> emission as a result of subsurface <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_testing" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuclear testing">nuclear explosions</a>. It has a half-life of 35 days.<sup id="cite_ref-iso_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iso-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Between locations in the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a>, the isotopic composition of argon varies greatly. Where the major source of argon is the decay of <a href="/wiki/Potassium-40" title="Potassium-40"><span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">40</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>K</span></a> in rocks, <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">40</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Ar</span> will be the dominant isotope, as it is on Earth. Argon produced directly by <a href="/wiki/Stellar_nucleosynthesis" title="Stellar nucleosynthesis">stellar nucleosynthesis</a>, is dominated by the <a href="/wiki/Alpha_process" title="Alpha process">alpha-process</a> nuclide <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">36</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Ar</span>. Correspondingly, solar argon contains 84.6% <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">36</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Ar</span> (according to <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">solar wind</a> measurements),<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> and the ratio of the three isotopes <sup>36</sup>Ar&#160;:&#160;<sup>38</sup>Ar&#160;:&#160;<sup>40</sup>Ar in the atmospheres of the outer planets is 8400&#160;:&#160;1600&#160;:&#160;1.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> This contrasts with the low abundance of <a href="/wiki/Primordial_nuclide" title="Primordial nuclide">primordial</a> <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">36</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Ar</span> in Earth's atmosphere, which is only 31.5 ppmv (= 9340 ppmv × 0.337%), comparable with that of neon (18.18 ppmv) on Earth and with interplanetary gasses, measured by <a href="/wiki/Interplanetary_probe" class="mw-redirect" title="Interplanetary probe">probes</a>.
</p><p>The atmospheres of <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Mars" title="Atmosphere of Mars">Mars</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">Mercury</a> and <a href="/wiki/Titan_(moon)" title="Titan (moon)">Titan</a> (the largest moon of <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a>) contain argon, predominantly as <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">40</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Ar</span>, and its content may be as high as 1.93% (Mars).<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The predominance of <a href="/wiki/Radiogenic" class="mw-redirect" title="Radiogenic">radiogenic</a> <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">40</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Ar</span> is the reason the <a href="/wiki/Standard_atomic_weight" title="Standard atomic weight">standard atomic weight</a> of terrestrial argon is greater than that of the next element, <a href="/wiki/Potassium" title="Potassium">potassium</a>, a fact that was puzzling when argon was discovered. <a href="/wiki/Dmitri_Mendeleev" title="Dmitri Mendeleev">Mendeleev</a> positioned the elements on his <a href="/wiki/Periodic_table" title="Periodic table">periodic table</a> in order of atomic weight, but the inertness of argon suggested a placement <i>before</i> the reactive <a href="/wiki/Alkali_metal" title="Alkali metal">alkali metal</a>. <a href="/wiki/Henry_Moseley" title="Henry Moseley">Henry Moseley</a> later solved this problem by showing that the periodic table is actually arranged in order of <a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">atomic number</a> (see <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_periodic_table" title="History of the periodic table">History of the periodic table</a>).
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Compounds">Compounds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Argon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Compounds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Argon_compounds" title="Argon compounds">Argon compounds</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Argon-fluorohydride-3D-vdW.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Argon-fluorohydride-3D-vdW.png/170px-Argon-fluorohydride-3D-vdW.png" width="170" height="114" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Argon-fluorohydride-3D-vdW.png/255px-Argon-fluorohydride-3D-vdW.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Argon-fluorohydride-3D-vdW.png/340px-Argon-fluorohydride-3D-vdW.png 2x" data-file-width="1100" data-file-height="739" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Argon-fluorohydride-3D-vdW.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Space-filling_model" title="Space-filling model">Space-filling model</a> of <a href="/wiki/Argon_fluorohydride" title="Argon fluorohydride">argon fluorohydride</a></div></div></div>
<p>Argon's complete octet of <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electrons</a> indicates full s and p subshells. This full <a href="/wiki/Valence_shell" class="mw-redirect" title="Valence shell">valence shell</a> makes argon very stable and extremely resistant to bonding with other elements. Before 1962, argon and the other noble gases were considered to be chemically inert and unable to form compounds; however, compounds of the heavier noble gases have since been synthesized. The first argon compound with tungsten pentacarbonyl, W(CO)<sub>5</sub>Ar, was isolated in 1975. However it was not widely recognised at that time.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> In August 2000, another argon compound, <a href="/wiki/Argon_fluorohydride" title="Argon fluorohydride">argon fluorohydride</a> (HArF), was formed by researchers at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Helsinki" title="University of Helsinki">University of Helsinki</a>, by shining ultraviolet light onto frozen argon containing a small amount of <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_fluoride" title="Hydrogen fluoride">hydrogen fluoride</a> with <a href="/wiki/Caesium_iodide" title="Caesium iodide">caesium iodide</a>. This discovery caused the recognition that argon could form weakly bound compounds, even though it was not the first.<sup id="cite_ref-sciencenews-harf_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sciencenews-harf-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> It is stable up to 17&#160;<a href="/wiki/Kelvin" title="Kelvin">kelvin</a><a href="/wiki/Kelvin#Usage_conventions" title="Kelvin">s</a> (−256&#160;°C). The <a href="/wiki/Metastable" class="mw-redirect" title="Metastable">metastable</a> <span class="chemf nowrap">ArCF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2+</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> dication, which is valence-<a href="/wiki/Isoelectronicity" title="Isoelectronicity">isoelectronic</a> with <a href="/wiki/Carbonyl_fluoride" title="Carbonyl fluoride">carbonyl fluoride</a> and <a href="/wiki/Phosgene" title="Phosgene">phosgene</a>, was observed in 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Argon-36" class="mw-redirect" title="Argon-36">Argon-36</a>, in the form of argon hydride (<a href="/wiki/Argonium" title="Argonium">argonium</a>) ions, has been detected in <a href="/wiki/Interstellar_medium" title="Interstellar medium">interstellar medium</a> associated with the <a href="/wiki/Crab_Nebula" title="Crab Nebula">Crab Nebula</a> <a href="/wiki/Supernova" title="Supernova">supernova</a>; this was the first <a href="/wiki/Noble_gas" title="Noble gas">noble-gas molecule</a> detected in <a href="/wiki/Outer_space" title="Outer space">outer space</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NYT-20131213_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20131213-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Solid argon <a href="/wiki/Hydride" title="Hydride">hydride</a> (Ar(H<sub>2</sub>)<sub>2</sub>) has the same crystal structure as the MgZn<sub>2</sub> <a href="/wiki/Laves_phase" title="Laves phase">Laves phase</a>. It forms at pressures between 4.3 and 220 GPa, though Raman measurements suggest that the H<sub>2</sub> molecules in Ar(H<sub>2</sub>)<sub>2</sub> dissociate above 175 GPa.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Production">Production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Argon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Industrial">Industrial</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Argon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Industrial">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Argon is produced industrially by the <a href="/wiki/Fractional_distillation" title="Fractional distillation">fractional distillation</a> of <a href="/wiki/Liquid_air" title="Liquid air">liquid air</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Cryogenic" class="mw-redirect" title="Cryogenic">cryogenic</a> <a href="/wiki/Air_separation" title="Air separation">air separation</a> unit; a process that separates <a href="/wiki/Liquid_nitrogen" title="Liquid nitrogen">liquid nitrogen</a>, which boils at 77.3&#160;K, from argon, which boils at 87.3&#160;K, and <a href="/wiki/Liquid_oxygen" title="Liquid oxygen">liquid oxygen</a>, which boils at 90.2&#160;K. About 700,000 <a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">tonnes</a> of argon are produced worldwide every year.<sup id="cite_ref-emsley_21-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-emsley-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_radioactive_decays">In radioactive decays</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Argon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: In radioactive decays">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_argon" title="Isotopes of argon"><sup>40</sup>Ar</a>, the most abundant <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotope</a> of argon, is produced by the decay of <sup>40</sup><a href="/wiki/Potassium" title="Potassium">K</a> with a half-life of 1.25<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup><span style="white-space:nowrap">9</span></sup> years by <a href="/wiki/Electron_capture" title="Electron capture">electron capture</a> or <a href="/wiki/Positron_emission" title="Positron emission">positron emission</a>. Because of this, it is used in <a href="/wiki/Potassium%E2%80%93argon_dating" class="mw-redirect" title="Potassium–argon dating">potassium–argon dating</a> to determine the age of rocks.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Applications">Applications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Argon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Applications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Argon.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Argon.jpg/180px-Argon.jpg" width="180" height="270" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Argon.jpg/270px-Argon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Argon.jpg/360px-Argon.jpg 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="768" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Argon.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cylinders containing argon gas for use in extinguishing fire without damaging server equipment</div></div></div>
<p>Argon has several desirable properties:
</p>
<ul><li>Argon is a chemically <a href="/wiki/Inert_gas" title="Inert gas">inert gas</a>.</li>
<li>Argon is the cheapest alternative when <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a> is not sufficiently inert.</li>
<li>Argon has low <a href="/wiki/Thermal_conductivity" title="Thermal conductivity">thermal conductivity</a>.</li>
<li>Argon has electronic properties (ionization and/or the emission spectrum) desirable for some applications.</li></ul>
<p>Other <a href="/wiki/Noble_gas" title="Noble gas">noble gases</a> would be equally suitable for most of these applications, but argon is by far the cheapest. Argon is inexpensive, since it occurs naturally in air and is readily obtained as a byproduct of <a href="/wiki/Cryogenic" class="mw-redirect" title="Cryogenic">cryogenic</a> <a href="/wiki/Air_separation" title="Air separation">air separation</a> in the production of <a href="/wiki/Liquid_oxygen" title="Liquid oxygen">liquid oxygen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Liquid_nitrogen" title="Liquid nitrogen">liquid nitrogen</a>: the primary constituents of air are used on a large industrial scale. The other noble gases (except <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a>) are produced this way as well, but argon is the most plentiful by far. The bulk of argon applications arise simply because it is inert and relatively cheap.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Industrial_processes">Industrial processes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Argon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Industrial processes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Argon is used in some high-temperature industrial processes where ordinarily non-reactive substances become reactive. For example, an argon atmosphere is used in graphite electric furnaces to prevent the graphite from burning.
</p><p>For some of these processes, the presence of nitrogen or oxygen gases might cause defects within the material. Argon is used in some types of <a href="/wiki/Arc_welding" title="Arc welding">arc welding</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Gas_metal_arc_welding" title="Gas metal arc welding">gas metal arc welding</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gas_tungsten_arc_welding" title="Gas tungsten arc welding">gas tungsten arc welding</a>, as well as in the processing of <a href="/wiki/Titanium" title="Titanium">titanium</a> and other reactive elements. An argon atmosphere is also used for growing crystals of <a href="/wiki/Silicon" title="Silicon">silicon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Germanium" title="Germanium">germanium</a>.
</p>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Shielding_gas" title="Shielding gas">shielding gas</a></div>
<p>Argon is used in the poultry industry to <a href="/wiki/Asphyxiant_gas" title="Asphyxiant gas">asphyxiate</a> birds, either for mass culling following disease outbreaks, or as a means of slaughter more humane than the electric bath. Argon is denser than air and displaces oxygen close to the ground during <a href="/wiki/Inert_gas_asphyxiation" title="Inert gas asphyxiation">gassing</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ShieldsRaj2010_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ShieldsRaj2010-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Its non-reactive nature makes it suitable in a food product, and since it replaces oxygen within the dead bird, argon also enhances shelf life.<sup id="cite_ref-FraquezaBarreto2009_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FraquezaBarreto2009-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Argon is sometimes used for <a href="/wiki/Gaseous_fire_suppression" title="Gaseous fire suppression">extinguishing fires</a> where valuable equipment may be damaged by water or foam.<sup id="cite_ref-SuKim2001_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SuKim2001-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Scientific_research">Scientific research</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Argon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Scientific research">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Liquid argon is used as the target for neutrino experiments and direct <a href="/wiki/Dark_matter" title="Dark matter">dark matter</a> searches. The interaction between the hypothetical <a href="/wiki/Weakly_interacting_massive_particles" title="Weakly interacting massive particles">WIMPs</a> and an argon nucleus produces <a href="/wiki/Scintillation_(physics)" title="Scintillation (physics)">scintillation</a> light that is detected by <a href="/wiki/Photomultiplier_tubes" class="mw-redirect" title="Photomultiplier tubes">photomultiplier tubes</a>. Two-phase detectors containing argon gas are used to detect the ionized electrons produced during the WIMP–nucleus scattering. As with most other liquefied noble gases, argon has a high scintillation light yield (about 51 photons/keV<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>), is transparent to its own scintillation light, and is relatively easy to purify. Compared to <a href="/wiki/Xenon" title="Xenon">xenon</a>, argon is cheaper and has a distinct scintillation time profile, which allows the separation of electronic recoils from nuclear recoils.  On the other hand, its intrinsic beta-ray background is larger due to <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">39</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Ar</span> contamination, unless one uses argon from underground sources, which has much less <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">39</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Ar</span> contamination. Most of the argon in the Earth’s atmosphere was produced by electron capture of long-lived <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">40</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>K</span> (<span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">40</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>K</span> + e<sup>−</sup> → <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">40</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Ar</span> + ν) present in natural potassium within the Earth.  The <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">39</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Ar</span> activity in the atmosphere is maintained by cosmogenic production through the knockout reaction <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">40</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Ar</span>(n,2n)<span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">39</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Ar</span> and similar reactions.  The half-life of <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">39</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Ar</span> is only 269&#160;years.  As a result, the underground Ar, shielded by rock and water, has much less <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">39</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Ar</span> contamination.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>  Dark-matter detectors currently operating with liquid argon include <a href="/wiki/DarkSide" title="DarkSide">DarkSide</a>, <a href="/wiki/WIMP_Argon_Programme" title="WIMP Argon Programme">WArP</a>, <a href="/wiki/ArDM" title="ArDM">ArDM</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cryogenic_Low-Energy_Astrophysics_with_Neon" title="Cryogenic Low-Energy Astrophysics with Neon">microCLEAN</a> and <a href="/wiki/DEAP" title="DEAP">DEAP</a>.  Neutrino experiments include <a href="/wiki/ICARUS_(experiment)" class="mw-redirect" title="ICARUS (experiment)">ICARUS</a> and <a href="/wiki/MicroBooNE" title="MicroBooNE">MicroBooNE</a>, both of which use high-purity liquid argon in a <a href="/wiki/Time_projection_chamber" title="Time projection chamber">time projection chamber</a> for fine grained three-dimensional imaging of neutrino interactions.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Preservative">Preservative</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Argon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Preservative">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:CsCrystals.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/CsCrystals.JPG/220px-CsCrystals.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/CsCrystals.JPG/330px-CsCrystals.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/CsCrystals.JPG/440px-CsCrystals.JPG 2x" data-file-width="4000" data-file-height="3000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:CsCrystals.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A sample of <a href="/wiki/Caesium" title="Caesium">caesium</a> is packed under argon to avoid reactions with air</div></div></div>
<p>Argon is used to displace oxygen- and moisture-containing air in packaging material to extend the shelf-lives of the contents (argon has the <a href="/wiki/E_numbers" class="mw-redirect" title="E numbers">European food additive code</a> E938). Aerial oxidation, hydrolysis, and other chemical reactions that degrade the products are retarded or prevented entirely. High-purity chemicals and pharmaceuticals are sometimes packed and sealed in argon.
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Winemaking" title="Winemaking">winemaking</a>, argon is used in a variety of activities to provide a barrier against oxygen at the liquid surface, which can spoil wine by fueling both microbial metabolism (as with <a href="/wiki/Acetic_acid_bacteria" title="Acetic acid bacteria">acetic acid bacteria</a>) and standard <a href="/wiki/Redox" title="Redox">redox</a> chemistry.
</p><p>Argon is sometimes used as the propellant in <a href="/wiki/Aerosol" title="Aerosol">aerosol</a> cans for such products as <a href="/wiki/Varnish" title="Varnish">varnish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Polyurethane" title="Polyurethane">polyurethane</a>, and paint, and to displace air when preparing a container for storage after opening.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Since 2002, the American <a href="/wiki/National_Archives" class="mw-redirect" title="National Archives">National Archives</a> stores important national documents such as the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Declaration_of_Independence" title="United States Declaration of Independence">Declaration of Independence</a> and the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Constitution" title="United States Constitution">Constitution</a> within argon-filled cases to inhibit their degradation. Argon is preferable to the helium that had been used in the preceding five decades, because helium gas escapes through the intermolecular pores in most containers and must be regularly replaced.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Laboratory_equipment">Laboratory equipment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Argon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Laboratory equipment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Glovebox.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Glovebox.jpg/220px-Glovebox.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Glovebox.jpg/330px-Glovebox.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Glovebox.jpg/440px-Glovebox.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1536" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Glovebox.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Glovebox" title="Glovebox">Gloveboxes</a> are often filled with argon, which recirculates over scrubbers to maintain an <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a>-, <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a>-, and moisture-free atmosphere</div></div></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Air-free_technique" title="Air-free technique">Air-free technique</a></div>
<p>Argon may be used as the <a href="/wiki/Inert_gas" title="Inert gas">inert gas</a> within <a href="/wiki/Schlenk_line" title="Schlenk line">Schlenk lines</a> and <a href="/wiki/Glovebox" title="Glovebox">gloveboxes</a>. Argon is preferred to less expensive nitrogen in cases where nitrogen may react with the reagents or apparatus.
</p><p>Argon may be used as the carrier gas in <a href="/wiki/Gas_chromatography" title="Gas chromatography">gas chromatography</a> and in <a href="/wiki/Electrospray_ionization_mass_spectrometry" class="mw-redirect" title="Electrospray ionization mass spectrometry">electrospray ionization mass spectrometry</a>; it is the gas of choice for the plasma used in <a href="/wiki/Inductively_coupled_plasma" title="Inductively coupled plasma">ICP</a> <a href="/wiki/Spectroscopy" title="Spectroscopy">spectroscopy</a>. Argon is preferred for the sputter coating of specimens for <a href="/wiki/Scanning_electron_microscopy" class="mw-redirect" title="Scanning electron microscopy">scanning electron microscopy</a>. Argon gas is also commonly used for <a href="/wiki/Sputter_deposition" title="Sputter deposition">sputter deposition</a> of thin films as in <a href="/wiki/Microelectronics" title="Microelectronics">microelectronics</a> and for <a href="/wiki/Microfabrication" title="Microfabrication">wafer cleaning in microfabrication</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Medical_use">Medical use</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Argon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Medical use">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Cryosurgery" title="Cryosurgery">Cryosurgery</a> procedures such as <a href="/wiki/Cryoablation" title="Cryoablation">cryoablation</a> use liquid argon to destroy tissue such as <a href="/wiki/Cancer" title="Cancer">cancer</a> cells. It is used in a procedure called "argon-enhanced coagulation", a form of argon <a href="/wiki/Plasma_torch" title="Plasma torch">plasma beam</a> <a href="/wiki/Electrosurgery" title="Electrosurgery">electrosurgery</a>. The procedure carries a risk of producing <a href="/wiki/Gas_embolism" class="mw-redirect" title="Gas embolism">gas embolism</a> and has resulted in the death of at least one patient.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Blue <a href="/wiki/Argon_laser" class="mw-redirect" title="Argon laser">argon lasers</a> are used in surgery to weld arteries, destroy tumors, and correct eye defects.<sup id="cite_ref-emsley_21-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-emsley-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Argon has also been used experimentally to replace nitrogen in the breathing or decompression mix known as <a href="/wiki/Argox_(breathing_gas)" class="mw-redirect" title="Argox (breathing gas)">Argox</a>, to speed the elimination of dissolved nitrogen from the blood.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lighting">Lighting</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Argon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Lighting">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ArTube.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/ArTube.jpg/170px-ArTube.jpg" width="170" height="170" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/ArTube.jpg/255px-ArTube.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/ArTube.jpg/340px-ArTube.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2000" data-file-height="2000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ArTube.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Argon <a href="/wiki/Gas-discharge_lamp" title="Gas-discharge lamp">gas-discharge lamp</a> forming the symbol for argon "Ar"</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Incandescent_light" class="mw-redirect" title="Incandescent light">Incandescent lights</a> are filled with argon, to preserve the <a href="/wiki/Electrical_filament" class="mw-redirect" title="Electrical filament">filaments</a> at high temperature from oxidation. It is used for the specific way it ionizes and emits light, such as in <a href="/wiki/Plasma_globe" title="Plasma globe">plasma globes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Calorimeter_(particle_physics)" title="Calorimeter (particle physics)">calorimetry</a> in experimental <a href="/wiki/Particle_physics" title="Particle physics">particle physics</a>. <a href="/wiki/Gas-discharge_lamp" title="Gas-discharge lamp">Gas-discharge lamps</a> filled with pure argon provide lilac/violet light; with argon and some mercury, blue light. Argon is also used for blue and green <a href="/wiki/Argon_laser" class="mw-redirect" title="Argon laser">argon-ion lasers</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Miscellaneous_uses">Miscellaneous uses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Argon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Miscellaneous uses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Argon is used for <a href="/wiki/Thermal_insulation" title="Thermal insulation">thermal insulation</a> in <a href="/wiki/Insulated_glazing" title="Insulated glazing">energy-efficient windows</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> Argon is also used in technical <a href="/wiki/Scuba_diving" title="Scuba diving">scuba diving</a> to inflate a <a href="/wiki/Dry_suit" title="Dry suit">dry suit</a> because it is inert and has low thermal conductivity.<sup id="cite_ref-IEEE2008_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IEEE2008-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Argon is used as a propellant in the development of the <a href="/wiki/Variable_Specific_Impulse_Magnetoplasma_Rocket" title="Variable Specific Impulse Magnetoplasma Rocket">Variable Specific Impulse Magnetoplasma Rocket</a> (VASIMR).  Compressed argon gas is allowed to expand, to cool the seeker heads of some versions of the <a href="/wiki/AIM-9_Sidewinder" title="AIM-9 Sidewinder">AIM-9 Sidewinder</a> missile and other missiles that use cooled thermal seeker heads. The gas is <a href="/wiki/AIM-9_Sidewinder#Design" title="AIM-9 Sidewinder">stored at high pressure</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Argon-39, with a half-life of 269 years, has been used for a number of applications, primarily <a href="/wiki/Ice_core" title="Ice core">ice core</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ground_water" class="mw-redirect" title="Ground water">ground water</a> dating. Also, <a href="/wiki/Potassium%E2%80%93argon_dating" class="mw-redirect" title="Potassium–argon dating">potassium–argon dating</a> and related <a href="/wiki/Argon_argon_dating" class="mw-redirect" title="Argon argon dating">argon-argon dating</a> is used to date <a href="/wiki/Sedimentary_rock" title="Sedimentary rock">sedimentary</a>, <a href="/wiki/Metamorphic_rock" title="Metamorphic rock">metamorphic</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Igneous_rock" title="Igneous rock">igneous rocks</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-emsley_21-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-emsley-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Argon has been used by athletes as a doping agent to simulate <a href="/wiki/Hypoxia_(environmental)" title="Hypoxia (environmental)">hypoxic</a> conditions.  In 2014, the <a href="/wiki/World_Anti-Doping_Agency" title="World Anti-Doping Agency">World Anti-Doping Agency</a> (WADA) added argon and <a href="/wiki/Xenon" title="Xenon">xenon</a> to the list of prohibited substances and methods, although at this time there is no reliable test for abuse.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Safety">Safety</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Argon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Safety">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Although argon is non-toxic, it is 38% <a href="/wiki/Density" title="Density">denser</a> than air and therefore considered a dangerous <a href="/wiki/Asphyxiant_gas" title="Asphyxiant gas">asphyxiant</a> in closed areas. It is difficult to detect because it is colorless, odorless, and tasteless. A 1994 incident, in which a man was <a href="/wiki/Asphyxia" title="Asphyxia">asphyxiated</a> after entering an argon-filled section of oil pipe under construction in <a href="/wiki/Alaska" title="Alaska">Alaska</a>, highlights the dangers of argon tank leakage in confined spaces and emphasizes the need for proper use, storage and handling.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Argon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Industrial_gas" title="Industrial gas">Industrial gas</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Oxygen%E2%80%93argon_ratio" title="Oxygen–argon ratio">Oxygen–argon ratio</a>, a ratio of two physically similar gases, which has importance in various sectors.</li></ul>
